###begin p 55
###xml 19 25 19 25 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 90 96 90 96 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 220 222 220 222 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">12</sub>
###xml 298 299 298 299 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig2" ref-type="fig">2</xref>
###xml 330 336 330 336 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 411 412 411 412 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig2" ref-type="fig">2</xref>
###xml 446 452 446 452 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
We also challenged pdr10∆ cells with a number of detergents. As judged by a halo assay, a pdr10∆ mutant strain in the W303 genetic background reproducibly exhibited modestly elevated resistance to detergents containing C12 alkyl chains, including dodecyl maltoside and dodecyl phosphocholine (Fig. 2b, upper two rows). However, a pdr10∆ mutant in the YPH499 genetic background did not exhibit this effect (Fig. 2b, lowest row). We therefore used pdr10∆ cells derived from the W303 lineage for further study.
###end p 55
###begin p 56
###xml 129 142 129 142 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 281 285 281 285 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR10">1998</xref>
###xml 300 304 300 304 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR71">2004</xref>
###xml 324 328 324 328 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR93">2000</xref>
###xml 438 442 438 442 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR57">2006</xref>
###xml 462 466 462 466 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR67">2006</xref>
###xml 484 485 484 485 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig2" ref-type="fig">2</xref>
###xml 493 499 493 499 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 674 678 674 678 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR90">1988</xref>
###xml 735 736 735 736 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig2" ref-type="fig">2</xref>
###xml 810 815 810 815 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">PDR10</italic>
###xml 961 967 961 967 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1337 1341 1337 1341 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR13">2004</xref>
###xml 1470 1474 1470 1474 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR107">2002</xref>
###xml 1498 1502 1498 1502 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR106">2003</xref>
###xml 1577 1583 1577 1583 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1643 1655 1643 1655 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710; chs3&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1660 1672 1660 1672 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710; chs6&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1738 1739 1738 1739 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig2" ref-type="fig">2</xref>
###xml 1786 1792 1786 1792 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1792 1797 1792 1797 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">chs3&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1846 1847 1846 1847 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig2" ref-type="fig">2</xref>
###xml 1972 1978 1972 1978 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 129 142 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 210 215 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 335 340 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
In addition to changes in membrane composition, changes in cell wall structure can affect the detergent sensitivity displayed by S. cerevisiae cells; indeed, cell wall alterations can affect the sensitivity of yeast cells even to a strong anionic detergent like SDS (Bickle et al. 1998; Machi et al. 2004; Santos and Snyder 2000). The yeast cell wall is composed of three types of polymers: mannoproteins, glucan, and chitin (Klis et al. 2006; Lesage and Bussey 2006). We found (Fig. 2c) that pdr10∆ mutants were abnormally sensitive to two agents, Calcofluor White and Congo Red, that inhibit the growth of cells that have abnormally high amounts of chitin (Roncero et al. 1988). In contrast, otherwise isogenic wild-type cells (Fig. 2c), or cells expressing either Pdr10-GFP or Pdr10-mh from the chromosomal PDR10 locus (data not shown), did not display such hypersensitivity (and, thus, both Pdr10-GFP and Pdr10-mh are functional). If the sensitivity of the pdr10∆ cells is due to abnormal chitin, then we expected that sensitivity to Calcofluor White might be ameliorated by compromising chitin production. The chitin synthase responsible for producing the majority of the chitin generated in a vegetatively growing cell is Chs3, but cells can survive in its absence due to the function of the homologous chitin synthase Chs2 (Cabib 2004). Chs3 is normally delivered to the plasma membrane via the action of dedicated escort factors, including Chs6 (Valdivia et al. 2002; Valdivia and Schekman 2003). Consistent with the conclusion that the Calcofluor White sensitivity of pdr10∆ cells arises from elevated cell wall chitin, we found that pdr10∆ chs3∆ and pdr10∆ chs6∆ double mutants were not hypersensitive to Calcofluor White (Fig. 2c). Likewise, in the W303 strain background, a pdr10∆chs3∆ double mutant was not detergent resistant (Fig. 2b, upper rows). These findings indicate that both the Calcofluor White sensitivity and the detergent resistance displayed by pdr10∆ cells are largely due to enhanced chitin production by the integral membrane protein Chs3 in the absence of this ABC transporter.
###end p 56
###begin p 57
###xml 134 147 134 147 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 307 311 307 311 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR83">1998</xref>
###xml 328 334 328 334 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr12&#8710;</italic>
###xml 485 489 485 489 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR83">1998</xref>
###xml 506 512 506 512 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 562 563 562 563 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig2" ref-type="fig">2</xref>
###xml 689 702 689 702 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710; pdr12&#8710;</italic>
###xml 723 724 723 724 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig2" ref-type="fig">2</xref>
###xml 134 147 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
Although Pdr10 shares its highest degree of homology with Pdr5 and Pdr15, it is also closely related to other ABC transporters in the S. cerevisiae genome, including Pdr12 (36% identity to Pdr10). Pdr12 is located in the plasma membrane and is responsible for the export of weak organic acids (Piper et al. 1998). Consequently, pdr12∆ mutants are hypersensitive to the growth inhibitory action of weak organic acids used as food preservatives (e.g., sorbate or benzoate) (Piper et al. 1998). We found that pdr10∆ cells exhibited weak resistance to sorbate (Fig. 2d); however, this behavior required the function of Pdr12, because the modest sorbate resistance observed was eliminated in a pdr10∆ pdr12∆ double mutant (Fig. 2d). Thus, here, too, the absence of Pdr10 somehow promoted the function of a sister integral membrane protein, Pdr12.
###end p 57

###xml 313 318 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 356 369 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
Taken together, these results suggested that the function of Pdr10 contributes to the normal expression, and/or sorting and trafficking, and/or catalytic competence of Chs3 and Pdr12. To determine whether this phenotype was unique to the loss of Pdr10, we examined the Calcofluor White and sorbate sensitivity of yeast strains lacking other members of the S. cerevisiae ABC transporter family known to reside in the plasma membrane. Although comparable sorbate resistance was observed in pdr15∆ cells and comparable Calcofluor White sensitivity was observed in cells lacking the putative, but uncharacterized, ABC transporter encoding by the YOL075c locus, the combination of Calcofluor White sensitivity and sorbate resistance was only observed in pdr10∆ cells (Fig. 3). Thus, Pdr10 uniquely has a role in maintaining the proper level, maturation, localization, and/or function of both Chs3 and Pdr12.Fig. 3Calcofluor sensitivity and sorbate resistance define a specific pdr10∆ phenotype. ###end p 58

###begin title 60

To distinguish among these possibilities, we first examined the effect of a pdr10∆ mutation on the function and localization of Chs3. To confirm that pdr10∆ cells do produce abnormally high levels of chitin, we examined cells by fluorescence microscopy after staining with Calcofluor White. Indeed, we found that, compared to otherwise isogenic PDR10+ control cells, pdr10∆ cells exhibited brighter fluorescence and therefore excess chitin, while cell wall chitin was almost entirely eliminated when Chs3 was absent (Fig. 4a). Elevated chitin production could arise from increased Chs3, more active Chs3, or aberrant localization of Chs3. As mentioned already, normal anterograde trafficking of Chs3 to the plasma membrane requires additional protein factors, including Chs6 (Valdivia and Schekman 2003), and we found that, like a chs3∆ mutation, a chs6∆ mutation also eliminated the Calcofluor White sensitivity of pdr10∆ cells (Fig. 2c). Therefore, the elevated chitin production of pdr10∆ cells does not occur because the normal pathway for transport of Chs3 to the plasma membrane is bypassed when Pdr10 is absent.Fig. 4Defective endocytosis of Chs3 causes excess chitin deposition in pdr10∆ cells. a Cells from log phase cultures of strains W303-1A (wild type), NRY243 (pdr10∆), NRY602 (chs3∆), and NRY601 (pdr10∆ chs3∆) were stained for chitin and examined by fluorescence microscopy. Bar, 4 mum. b Log phase cells from strains W303-1A and NRY251 (pdr10∆) carrying plasmids pRS315-CHS7 and pCRP12 were treated with 3.6 muM alpha-factor for 35 min in SCD buffered to pH 3.5 with 25 mM sodium succinate and supplemented with adenine. Pheromone was then washed out at time 0, and the localization of Chs3-GFP was monitored by fluorescence microscopy as described under Materials and Methods. Punctate Chs3-GFP staining of the plasma membrane in pdr10∆ cells at late time points is indicated by arrow heads. Bar, 3 mum. c Strains W303-1A, NRY243, and NRY407 (cho1∆) were grown into log phase, stained for chitin, and examined by fluorescence microscopy. Bar, 5 mum. d Serial dilutions of overnight cultures of strains W303-1A, NRY243, and NRY407 were spotted onto YPD plates containing Calcofluor

###begin p 63
###xml 149 153 149 153 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR107">2002</xref>
###xml 270 274 270 274 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR17">1996</xref>
###xml 481 487 481 487 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 604 605 604 605 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig4" ref-type="fig">4</xref>
###xml 696 702 696 702 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 843 849 843 849 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1040 1046 1040 1046 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
In vegetatively growing cells, Chs3 cycles between the trans-Golgi network and endosomal compartments during most of the cell cycle (Valdivia et al. 2002) but displays significant accumulation at the plasma membrane primarily at the small-bud stage (Chuang and Schekman 1996). The intracellular pool of Chs3 can also be redistributed to the plasma membrane after treatment of cells with mating pheromone, an effect we exploited to trap Chs3 at the plasma membrane in wild-type and pdr10∆ cells. Upon washout of pheromone, cells re-entered the cell cycle and Chs3 moved to the bud neck, as expected (Fig. 4b). At later time points, Chs3 was efficiently internalized in wild-type cells, whereas in pdr10∆ cells a small amount of Chs3-GFP persisted in discrete patches at the plasma membrane, even though the majority was intracellular. Thus, in pdr10∆ cells, endocytosis of Chs3 was somewhat less efficient than in wild-type cells (although this block was by no means complete). The prolonged residence time of Chs3 at the plasma membrane in pdr10∆ cells could well explain the increased amount of chitin produced in these cells.
###end p 63
###begin p 64
###xml 74 80 74 80 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>

###xml 428 434 428 434 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>

###xml 854 855 854 855 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig5" ref-type="fig">5</xref>
###xml 858 864 858 864 <label xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Fig.&#160;5</label>
###xml 928 929 928 929 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">a</bold>

###xml 685 690 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
We were interested in determining whether this modest endocytic defect of pdr10∆ cells was specific for Chs3 or was more general. We therefore examined endocytic uptake of two fluorescent dyes, Lucifer Yellow and FM 4-64 (Fig. 5a). These dyes serve as markers for fluid phase endocytosis and bulk membrane internalization, respectively, with both being internalized to the vacuole. Uptake of both of these markers was normal in pdr10∆ cells, demonstrating that the partial block in Chs3 endocytosis is not a general effect on all endocytic processes. We also examined actin morphology in these cells (Fig. 5b), because an intact actin cytoskeleton is a prerequisite for endocytosis in yeast (Ayscough et al. 1997; Kubler and Riezman 1993). Deletion of PDR10 had no apparent effect on actin localization or on its polarization in small-budded cells (Fig. 5b).Fig. 5Pdr10 is not required for bulk endocytosis or actin morphology. a Strains W303-1A (wild type) and NRY243 (pdr10∆) were grown to log phase, incubated with Lucifer Yellow or FM 4-64 as a marker for endocytosis, and examined by fluorescence microscopy. b Strains W303-1A and NRY243 were grown to log phase, permeabilized, and stained with rhodamine-phalloidin to visualize actin by fluorescence microscopy. All bars, 5 mum

If the partial defect in endocytosis of Chs3 in a pdr10∆ mutant is responsible for its elevated chitin production and thus its increased sensitivity to Calcofluor White, then other mutations or conditions that influence the efficiency of Chs3 endocytosis should elicit the same phenotype. Interestingly, we found that a cho1∆ strain, which lacks phosphatidylserine synthase (Atkinson et al. 1980a, b; Kiyono et al. 1987; Kohlwein et al. 1988; Letts et al. 1983), displayed even greater chitin deposition (Fig. 4c) and an even higher sensitivity to Calcofluor White than the pdr10∆ strain (Fig. 4d). Quantitation of chitin deposition indicated that pdr10∆ cells had approximately 1.9-fold more chitin in their cell walls than wild-type cells, while cho1∆ cells had approximately 3.8-fold more chitin. We have not yet examined directly the localization of Chs3-GFP in cho1∆ cells; however, these data suggested that membrane composition might be important in Chs3 endocytosis and thus that the absence of Pdr10 might influence plasma membrane lipid distribution. We therefore examined sterol localization in wildtype and pdr10∆ cells by staining with filipin (Fig. 6a), finding little difference between those two strains. Simons and colleagues have reported that treatment with mating pheromone polarizes sterols to the site of polarized growth (the shmoo tip) (Bagnat and Simons 2002), and we find that this process proceeds normally in pdr10∆ cells as well (Fig. 6a).Fig. 6Increased phosphatidylethanolamine is accessible to TNBS labeling in pdr10∆ cells. a Strains W303-1A and NRY243 were grown to log phase, incubated with or without alpha-factor, and then stained with filipin to visualize sterols by fluorescence microscopy. Bar, 5 mum. b Exposure of endogenous aminophospholipids to TNBS was assayed with cells grown overnight in the presence of 32P-phosphate as described under Materials and Methods. Strains used were W303-1A (wild type), NRY243 (pdr10∆), NRY549 (lem3∆), NRY551 (pdr10∆ lem3∆), NRY427 (dnf1∆ dnf2∆), and NRY558 (pdr10∆ dnf1∆ dnf2∆)
###end p 67

###begin p 69
###xml 229 233 229 233 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR84">2003</xref>
###xml 246 258 246 258 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr5&#8710; pdr15&#8710;</italic>
###xml 355 359 355 359 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR95">2007</xref>
###xml 535 539 535 539 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR38">1973</xref>
###xml 557 561 557 561 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR84">2003</xref>
###xml 579 583 579 583 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR99">1998</xref>
###xml 742 746 742 746 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR84">2003</xref>
###xml 803 804 803 804 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab2" ref-type="table">2</xref>
###xml 860 861 860 861 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig6" ref-type="fig">6</xref>
###xml 881 887 881 887 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 903 905 903 905 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">32</sup>
###xml 934 940 934 940 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1019 1020 1019 1020 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab2" ref-type="table">2</xref>
###xml 1126 1127 1126 1127 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig6" ref-type="fig">6</xref>
###xml 1137 1143 1137 1143 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
It has been proposed that proper maintenance of membrane asymmetry is important for endocytosis at low temperatures and that Pdr5 and another ABC transporter, Yor1, are candidate outward-directed PE translocases (Pomorski et al. 2003). Moreover, pdr5∆ pdr15∆ cells exhibit increased accessibility of PE to the small-molecule reagent TNBS (Schuller et al. 2007), which is able to pass through the cell wall. TNBS labeling has been used to assess exposure of PE and PS on the outer leaflet of the plasma membrane (Gordesky and Marinetti 1973; Pomorski et al. 2003; Siegmund et al. 1998), although subcellular fractionation data raise the possibility that there is considerable leakage of the reagent into the secretory pathway (Pomorski et al. 2003). We analyzed the total phospholipid composition (Table 2) and accessibility of aminophospholipids to TNBS (Fig. 6b) in wild-type and pdr10∆ cells grown on 32P-phosphate. We found that a pdr10∆ mutation had no discernible effect on overall phospholipid composition (Table 2), but caused a significant increase in the fraction of the PE that was accessible to TNBS labeling (Fig. 6b). Thus, pdr10∆ plasma membranes either are significantly more permeable to TNBS or exhibit a defect in maintenance of plasma membrane asymmetry.
###end p 69
###begin p 70
###xml 222 226 222 226 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR8">1994</xref>
###xml 250 254 250 254 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR11">1994</xref>
###xml 276 280 276 280 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR21">1995</xref>
###xml 295 299 295 299 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR29">1998</xref>
###xml 313 318 313 318 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR72">1996a</xref>
###xml 336 340 336 340 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR84">2003</xref>
###xml 358 362 358 362 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR95">2007</xref>
###xml 651 657 651 657 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 781 787 781 787 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 912 916 912 916 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">DNF1</italic>
###xml 921 926 921 926 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">DNF2,</italic>
###xml 946 947 946 947 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig6" ref-type="fig">6</xref>
###xml 1008 1012 1008 1012 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">LEM3</italic>
###xml 1150 1154 1150 1154 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR91">2004</xref>
###xml 1225 1237 1225 1237 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710; lem3&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1241 1259 1241 1259 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710; dnf1&#8710; dnf2&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1274 1275 1274 1275 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig6" ref-type="fig">6</xref>
###xml 1370 1371 1370 1371 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab2" ref-type="table">2</xref>
###xml 1431 1436 1431 1436 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dnf1&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1436 1441 1436 1441 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dnf2&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1446 1451 1446 1451 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">lem3&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1468 1474 1468 1474 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1500 1506 1500 1506 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1519 1531 1519 1531 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dnf1&#8710; dnf2&#8710;.</italic>
The ABC transporters with established transport activities that are most closely related to Pdr10, including Pdr5 and Snq2, mediate outward-directed lipid transport and/or export of xenobiotics from the cell (Balzi et al. 1994; Bissinger and Kuchler 1994; Decottignies et al. 1995; Egner et al. 1998; Mahe et al. 1996a; Pomorski et al. 2003; Schuller et al. 2007). If Pdr10 had a similar function, then loss of Pdr10 should reduce, not increase, the fraction of PE that is surface-exposed. In contrast, if the absence of Pdr10 somehow indirectly crippled the function of the inward-directed PE translocases, then the biochemical phenotype observed in pdr10∆ cells would be more readily explained. In support of this, we observed that the level of PE accessible to TNBS labeling in pdr10∆ cells was comparable to that in cells in which the genes for both putative inward-directed plasma membrane PE translocases, DNF1 and DNF2, were deleted (Fig. 6b); similar results were also obtained for cells lacking the LEM3 gene, which encodes an integral membrane protein that is required for exit of Dnf1 and Dnf2 from the endoplasmic reticulum (Saito et al. 2004). Furthermore, no additive increase in exposure of PE was observed in pdr10∆ lem3∆ or pdr10∆ dnf1∆ dnf2∆ strains (Fig. 6b). The overall phospholipid compositions of all of these strains were quite comparable (Table 2). The absence of any additive or synergistic effect of the dnf1∆dnf2∆ (or lem3∆) mutations in a pdr10∆ mutant demonstrated that pdr10∆ phenocopied dnf1∆ dnf2∆. That is, regardless of whether TNBS labeling is measuring PE exposure on the outer leaflet or permeability, the loss of Pdr10 function mimicked the loss of Dnf1 and Dnf2 function in these cells.
###end p 70

We were interested in whether this might reflect a direct role for Pdr10 in the synthesis, sorting, or activity of the inward-directed translocases. Therefore, we examined the level of expression and the localization of Dnf1, Dnf2, and Lem3 in pdr10∆ cells by constructing functional chromosomal GFP fusions (tests for functionality described under Materials and Methods). Dnf1-GFP, Dnf2-GFP, and Lem3-GFP were all readily detectable by fluorescence microscopy, present at equivalent levels, and exhibited the expected punctate pattern in the plasma membrane in both wild-type and pdr10∆ cells (Fig. 7). There were no discernible differences in the localization of any of these three proteins between wild-type and pdr10∆ cells. Likewise, a functional Pdr5-GFP chimera expressed from the chromosomal PDR5 locus was localized to the plasma membrane indistinguishably in wild-type and pdr10∆ cells (Fig. 7). Putative inward- and outward-directed translocases were thus present at normal levels and were properly localized to the plasma membrane in pdr10∆ cells.Fig. 7Pdr10 is not required for normal localization of putative PE translocases. ###xml 511 517 511 517 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 934 938 934 938 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR4">1997</xref>
###xml 956 960 956 960 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR84">2003</xref>
###xml 977 983 977 983 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1039 1050 1039 1050 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dnf1&#8710; dnf2&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1137 1138 1137 1138 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig8" ref-type="fig">8</xref>
###xml 1268 1272 1268 1272 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR70">2007</xref>
###xml 1296 1301 1296 1301 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">lem3&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1583 1589 1583 1589 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1594 1599 1594 1599 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">lem3&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1676 1677 1676 1677 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig8" ref-type="fig">8</xref>
###xml 1780 1786 1780 1786 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1825 1831 1825 1831 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 2040 2044 2040 2044 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR16">2006</xref>
###xml 2061 2065 2061 2065 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR82">2006</xref>
###xml 2142 2148 2142 2148 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 2187 2193 2187 2193 <label xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Fig.&#160;8</label>
###xml 2193 2199 2193 2199 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 2290 2296 2290 2296 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 2307 2312 2307 2312 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">lem3&#8710;</italic>
###xml 2323 2335 2323 2335 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710; lem3&#8710;</italic>
###xml 2346 2357 2346 2357 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dnf1&#8710; dnf2&#8710;</italic>
###xml 2372 2390 2372 2390 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710; dnf1&#8710; dnf2&#8710;</italic>
###xml 2553 2574 2553 2574 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Sec1" ref-type="sec">Materials and Methods</xref>
###xml 2193 2690 2193 2689 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" textid="74"><italic>pdr10&#8710;</italic> cells internalize NBD-PE in a Lem3-dependent manner. ###xml 2193 2690 2193 2689 <caption xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><p textid="74"><italic>pdr10&#8710;</italic> cells internalize NBD-PE in a Lem3-dependent manner. ###xml 2690 2690 2689 2689 <graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" position="anchor" xlink:href="232_2009_9173_Fig8_HTML" id="MO8"/>
###xml 2187 2690 2187 2689 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" id="Fig8"><label>Fig.&#160;8</label><caption><p textid="74"><italic>pdr10&#8710;</italic> cells internalize NBD-PE in a Lem3-dependent manner. Cells were examined by fluorescence microscopy and differential interference contrast microscopy (DIC). Bar, 6&#160;&#956;m</p></caption><graphic position="anchor" xlink:href="232_2009_9173_Fig8_HTML" id="MO8"/></fig>
To assess whether inward-directed translocase activity was still present in pdr10∆ cells, we examined the uptake of an exogenously supplied fluorescent PE surrogate, NBD-PE (Kean et al. 1993), in wild-type and pdr10∆ cells (Fig. 8). It has been reported previously that it is possible to observe specific, translocase-dependent internalization of NBD-labeled lipids at low temperature, which prevented background internalization due to endocytosis (Grant et al. 2001). However, in our hands, both wild-type and pdr10∆ cells were still able to internalize modest amounts of NBD-PE to discrete intracellular puncta at low temperature, indicating either that endocytosis was not completely blocked in our hands or that a low level of membrane leakage was occurring (data not shown). We therefore examined NBD-PE uptake in the presence of latrunculin A, which disrupts the actin cytoskeleton and hence blocks endocytosis (Ayscough et al. 1997; Pomorski et al. 2003). We found that pdr10∆ cells exhibited no defect compared to wild-type cells. dnf1∆ dnf2∆ cells also exhibited no defect in NBD-PE internalization under these conditions (Fig. 8), perhaps because the homologous P-type ATPase Drs2 can relocalize to the plasma membrane upon latrunculin treatment (Liu et al. 2007). Strikingly, however, lem3∆ cells were unable to internalize NBD-PE under these conditions, with or without the presence of Pdr10. These data demonstrate that Lem3 must have functions other than its role in the biogenesis of Dnf1 and Dnf2, perhaps in forward transport of Drs2 to the plasma membrane. Neither pdr10∆ nor lem3∆ had any effect on uptake of a fluorescent ceramide surrogate, NBD-Cer (Fig. 8). Taken together, these results demonstrate the presence of normal amounts of Dnf1, Dnf2, and Lem3 in pdr10∆ plasma membranes and demonstrate that pdr10∆ cells are still capable of PE internalization. It is known that mutations in a number of genes implicated in vesicular trafficking produce altered plasma membrane asymmetry as a secondary effect (Chen et al. 2006; Parsons et al. 2006), so it seems likely that increased accessibility of PE to TNBS labeling in pdr10∆ cells is such an indirect consequence.Fig. 8pdr10∆ cells internalize NBD-PE in a Lem3-dependent manner. Strains W303-1A (wild type), NRY243 (pdr10∆), NRY549 (lem3∆), NRY551 (pdr10∆ lem3∆), NRY427 (dnf1∆ dnf2∆), and NRY558 (pdr10∆ dnf1∆ dnf2∆) were grown to log phase, treated with latrunculin A to block endocytosis, and incubated with the fluorescent reporter lipid NBD-PE or NBD-Cer as described under Materials and Methods. Cells were examined by fluorescence microscopy and differential interference contrast microscopy (DIC). Bar, 6 mum
###end p 73

Dnf1 and Dnf2 have been implicated in endocytosis at low temperatures (Furuta et al. 2007; Pomorski et al. 2003), in keeping with a broader role for members of this transporter family in vesicular trafficking (Chen et al. 2006). Given that the PE exposure caused by a pdr10∆ mutation was quite comparable to that caused by dnf1∆ dnf2∆ double deletion, the mild defect in endocytic trafficking we observed for Chs3 in pdr10∆ cells might also be associated with dnf1∆ dnf2∆ or lem3∆ cells. Indeed, we found that cells lacking both Dnf1 and Dnf2 were sensitive to Calcofluor White (Fig. 9a), exhibited increased chitin in their cell wall (Fig. 9b), and were equivalent to pdr10∆ cells in both respects. Moreover, a pdr10∆ dnf1∆ dnf2∆ triple mutant strain did not display any increase in Calcofluor sensitivity or chitin deposition compared to the otherwise isogenic pdr10∆ single mutant or the dnf1∆ dnf2∆ double mutant (Fig. 9a, b), with all mutant strains exhibiting a 1.8- to 1.9-fold increase in chitin deposition. This lack of synergy was not due to any limitation in the capacity of these assays to detect such potentiation, because cho1∆ cells exhibited much higher Calcofluor White sensitivity and chitin deposition than pdr10∆ cells (Fig. 4). Moreover, lem3∆ cells were also more sensitive to Calcofluor White than pdr10∆ or dnf1∆ dnf2∆ cells (Fig. 9a), which is reminiscent of the more profound effect of this mutation on NBD-PE uptake (Fig. 8). Taken together, these results show that pdr10∆ cells exhibit a modest defect in endocytosis of Chs3, that this mimics a phenotype of dnf1∆ dnf2∆ cells, and that Pdr10 is not required for expression or localization of Dnf1, Dnf2, and Lem3. We have not been able to elucidate the molecular basis for this defect in Chs3 trafficking.Fig. 9Loss of Dnf1 and Dnf2 function in pdr10∆ cells is associated with their increased chitin deposition. a Serial dilutions from overnight cultures of strains W303-1A (wild type), NRY243 (pdr10∆), NRY427 (dnf1∆ dnf2∆), NRY558 (pdr10∆ dnf1∆ dnf2∆), NRY549 (lem3∆), and NRY551 (pdr10∆ lem3∆) were spotted onto YPD plates at increasing concentrations of Calcofluor White (CW). b Log phase cultures of strains W303-1A, NRY243, NRY427, and NRY558 were stained for chitin and examined by fluorescence microscopy. Bar, 5 mum. c Serial dilutions of strains W303-1A, NRY243, NRY549, and NRY551 were spotted onto YPD plates buffered to pH 3.5 with sodium succinate and containing potassium sorbate. d Serial dilutions of strains W303-1A, NRY243, NRY301 (pdr12∆), and NRY366 (pdr10∆ pdr12∆) were spotted onto YPD plates containing Calcofluor White
###end p 75

###begin title 77
Complex Interplay Among Pdr10, Lem3, and Pdr12
###end title 77
###begin p 78
###xml 133 134 133 134 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig2" ref-type="fig">2</xref>
###xml 197 208 197 208 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dnf1&#8710; dnf2&#8710;</italic>
###xml 218 223 218 223 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">lem3&#8710;</italic>
###xml 269 274 269 274 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">lem3&#8710;</italic>
###xml 320 325 320 325 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">lem3&#8710;</italic>
###xml 393 399 393 399 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 447 458 447 458 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dnf1&#8710; dnf2&#8710;</italic>
###xml 479 480 479 480 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig9" ref-type="fig">9</xref>
###xml 609 620 609 620 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dnf1&#8710; dnf2&#8710;</italic>
###xml 624 629 624 629 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">lem3&#8710;</italic>
Cells lacking Pdr10 also exhibit a modest increase in their resistance to sorbate that requires the sorbate transporter, Pdr12 (Fig. 2d). We were interested in whether this was also a phenotype of dnf1∆ dnf2∆ cells or lem3∆ cells. Interestingly, we found not only that lem3∆ cells were not sorbate resistant, but that a lem3∆ mutation eliminated the modest sorbate resistance conferred by the pdr10∆ mutation; no such epistasis was found with the dnf1∆ dnf2∆ double mutant (Fig. 9c and data not shown). Thus, the apparent increase in Pdr12 action found in cells lacking Pdr10 does not mimic a defect found in dnf1∆ dnf2∆ or lem3∆ cells but, rather, requires the presence of functional Lem3.
###end p 78
###begin p 79
###xml 103 104 103 104 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig2" ref-type="fig">2</xref>
###xml 179 180 179 180 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig9" ref-type="fig">9</xref>
###xml 238 244 238 244 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 327 333 327 333 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 340 341 340 341 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig9" ref-type="fig">9</xref>
###xml 386 392 386 392 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr12&#8710;</italic>
###xml 407 413 407 413 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 490 491 490 491 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab2" ref-type="table">2</xref>
Cells lacking Pdr10 exhibit increased sensitivity to the chitin-directed agent, Calcofluor White (Fig. 2c), and that is not further enhanced by the absence of Dnf1 and Dnf2 (Fig. 9a). Strikingly, as observed for the sorbate resistance of pdr10∆ cells, we found that loss of Pdr12 eliminated the Calcofluor White sensitivity of pdr10∆ (Fig. 9d). We also found that the introduction of a pdr12∆ mutation into pdr10∆ cells substantially reduced the accessibility of PE to TNBS labeling (Table 2). Collectively, these findings indicate complex interactions among Pdr10, Pdr12, and Lem3, which produce secondary effects on Chs3 endocytosis.
###end p 79
###begin title 80
Pdr10 Action Excludes Pdr12 from the Detergent-Resistant Membrane Fraction
###end title 80
###begin p 81
###xml 324 330 324 330 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 519 525 519 525 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 609 611 609 611 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig10" ref-type="fig">10</xref>
###xml 672 677 672 677 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">PDR12</italic>
###xml 701 707 701 707 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 770 776 770 776 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 796 798 796 798 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig10" ref-type="fig">10</xref>
###xml 888 894 888 894 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1192 1197 1192 1197 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">PDR12</italic>
###xml 1206 1212 1206 1212 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1250 1255 1250 1255 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">PDR10</italic>
###xml 1255 1256 1255 1256 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">+</italic>
###xml 1255 1256 1255 1256 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>+</italic></sup>
###xml 1458 1464 1458 1464 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1583 1589 1583 1589 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1755 1761 1755 1761 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1842 1849 1842 1849 <label xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Fig.&#160;10</label>
###xml 1849 1855 1849 1855 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1910 1911 1910 1911 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">a</bold>
###xml 2009 2015 2009 2015 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 2117 2118 2117 2118 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">b</bold>
###xml 2158 2164 2158 2164 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1849 2365 1849 2364 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" textid="82"><italic>pdr10&#8710;</italic> cells accumulate excess Pdr12 in the plasma membrane. <bold>a</bold> Total membranes were prepared from log phase cultures of strains W303-1A (wild type) and NRY243 (<italic>pdr10&#8710;</italic>). ###xml 1842 2365 1842 2364 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" id="Fig10"><label>Fig.&#160;10</label><caption><p textid="82"><italic>pdr10&#8710;</italic> cells accumulate excess Pdr12 in the plasma membrane. <bold>a</bold> Total membranes were prepared from log phase cultures of strains W303-1A (wild type) and NRY243 (<italic>pdr10&#8710;</italic>). Bar, 6&#160;&#956;m</p></caption><graphic position="anchor" xlink:href="232_2009_9173_Fig10_HTML" id="MO10"/></fig>
To see whether a clearer molecular function could be assigned to Pdr10, we examined whether loss of Pdr10 affects the level and/or localization of Pdr12. When equivalent amounts of plasma membrane protein were resolved by SDS-PAGE and analyzed by immunoblotting, we found that, compared to otherwise isogenic control cells, pdr10∆ mutant cells exhibited a reproducible, approximately twofold increase in the amount of Pdr12 relative to wild-type cells, whereas there was no significant difference between wild-type and pdr10∆ cells in the content of numerous other membrane proteins in the same samples (Fig. 10a). Visualization of functional Pdr12-GFP expressed from the PDR12 locus in wild-type and pdr10∆ cells confirmed that Pdr12-GFP expression was elevated in the pdr10∆ mutant cells (Fig. 10b), but we could distinguish no obvious difference in localization. In both wild-type and pdr10∆ cells, the Pdr12-GFP was observed throughout the plasma membrane in a readily observable punctate pattern. Approximately 30% of cells in both strains also exhibited some intracellular punctate staining (data not shown), with most such cells also exhibiting plasma membrane Pdr12-GFP. The level of PDR12 mRNA in pdr10∆ cells was not different from that in PDR10+ control cells, as judged by global transcriptome analysis using hybridization to DNA microarrays (S. Shtivelman, N. C. Rockwell, and J. Thorner, unpublished data). Thus, the increase in Pdr12 found in pdr10∆ cells could not be attributed to any change in transcription, and therefore the elevation of plasma membrane Pdr12 in pdr10∆ cells most likely results from a reduced rate of turnover rather than a faster rate of synthesis. The latter might occur if compartmentation of Pdr12 was altered in pdr10∆ cells in a manner that was not revealed at the level of fluorescence microscopy.Fig. 10pdr10∆ cells accumulate excess Pdr12 in the plasma membrane. a Total membranes were prepared from log phase cultures of strains W303-1A (wild type) and NRY243 (pdr10∆). The indicated proteins were detected by immunoblotting, with equal protein loaded for each strain. b Strains NRY915 (wild type) and NRY925 (pdr10∆) were grown to log phase in YPD, and Pdr12-GFP was examined by fluorescence microscopy. A collage of representative images is shown. Pdr12-GFP function was confirmed by sorbate sensitivity. Bar, 6 mum
###end p 81

When membranes are subjected to biochemical fractionation, a readily detectable amount of Pdr10 is associated with the DRM fraction (Fig. 1d). Hence, we also examined the distribution of Pdr12 in this assay. Strikingly, we found that approximately 60% of the total pool of Pdr12 was associated with the DRM fraction in membranes from pdr10∆ cells, whereas in the membranes from wild-type cells approximately one-third of the total Pdr12 was present in the DRM fraction and more than 50% was present in the detergent-soluble fraction (Fig. 11 and Table 3). The Pdr10-dependent shift observed for Pdr12 was in contrast to Chs3 and Gas1 (Bagnat et al. 2000; Nuoffer et al. 1991), whose distributions were unaltered by loss of Pdr10 (Fig. 11 and Table 3). Association of Lem3 and Pdr5 with the DRM fraction was slightly higher in pdr10∆ cells than in the wild type (Table 3), but this effect was too small to be statistically significant. Thus, our data indicate a novel role for Pdr10 as a negative regulator of the partitioning of Pdr12 into the DRM fraction; that is, Pdr10 must regulate the local environment of Pdr12 in some manner that alters its solubilization properties in the presence of detergent.Fig. 11pdr10∆ cells accumulate Pdr12 in the detergent-resistant membrane fraction. a Total membranes from log phase cultures of strains W303-1A or NRY904 (wildtype) and NRY243 or NRY908 (pdr10∆) were examined by cold detergent extraction, Optiprep density gradient flotation, and immunoblotting for the indicated proteins. Strains NRY904 and NRY908 contain Lem3 C-terminally tagged with a tandem myc-His6 (mh) tag. In vivo function of the resulting Lem3-mh protein was confirmed by Calcofluor sensitivity. b Immunoblotting data were quantitated, and the percentage of total Pdr12 (left) and Chs3 (right) associated with detergent-resistant and detergent-soluble fractions is presented as the mean value for four independent experiments, with error bars representing the standard deviation. Detergent-resistant and detergent-soluble membranes were defined as fractions 1-3 and 7-9, respectively, in the Optiprep density gradientsTable 3Association of membrane proteins with the detergent-resistant membrane fractionaProteinWild typepdr10∆% DRM% Soluble% DRM% SolublePdr1233 +/- 1154 +/- 1061 +/- 628 +/- 11Chs317 +/- 962 +/- 619 +/- 956 +/- 10Lem3-mh19 +/- 575 +/- 724 +/- 472 +/- 1Gas147 +/- 537 +/- 145 +/- 236 +/- 2Pdr557 +/- 1123 +/- 1069 +/- 914 +/- 8Note: Membranes were prepared and analyzed by cold Triton X-100 extraction, Optiprep density gradient flotation, and quantitative immunoblotting as described under Materials and Methods. Strains were W303-1A or NRY904 (wild type with or without chromosomally mh-tagged Lem3) and NRY243 or NRY908 (pdr10∆ with or without chromosomally mh-tagged Lem3)

We were also interested in whether Pdr10 function was redundant with that of its two closest homologues, Pdr5 and Pdr15. As one means to examine possible redundancy of this sort among Pdr10, Pdr5, and Pdr15, we examined their genetic interactions using Calcofluor White sensitivity and sorbate resistance. A pdr5∆ mutation eliminated both the Calcofluor White sensitivity and the mild sorbate resistance of pdr10∆ cells (Fig. 12a). pdr5∆ pdr10∆ cells also exhibited significantly lower TNBS labeling of PE (Table 2) and normal levels of Pdr12 (Fig. 12c). In contrast, a pdr15∆ mutation had no discernible effect on pdr10∆ phenotypes (Fig. 12a).Fig. 12Pdr5 and phosphosphingolipids are required for pdr10∆ phenotypes. a Serial dilutions of overnight cultures of strains W303-1A (wild type), NRY243 (pdr10∆), NRY201 (pdr5∆), NRY230 (pdr5∆ pdr10∆), NRY212 (pdr15∆), NRY236 (pdr10∆ pdr15∆), NRY410 (ipt1∆), NRY534 (pdr10∆ ipt1∆), NRY409 (sur1∆), and NRY526 (pdr10∆ sur1∆) were spotted onto YPD plates containing Calcofluor White (CW; left column) or sorbate buffered with sodium succinate (right column). b Strains W303-1A, NRY243, NRY409, and NRY526 were assayed for PE accessibility to TNBS labeling after overnight labeling with 32P-phosphate as described under Materials and Methods. c Total Pdr12 and Chs3 levels were examined by immunoblotting equal amounts of total membrane proteins isolated from log phase cultures of strains W303-1A, NRY230, and NRY526. d Strains W303-1A, NRY243, NRY409, NRY526, NRY410, and NRY534 were grown to log phase, stained for chitin, and examined by fluorescence microscopy. Bar, 5 mum
###end p 87
###begin p 88
###xml 47 53 47 53 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 66 67 66 67 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">a</bold>
###xml 147 153 147 153 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 164 169 164 169 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr5&#8710;</italic>
###xml 180 192 180 192 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr5&#8710; pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 203 209 203 209 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr15&#8710;</italic>
###xml 220 233 220 233 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710; pdr15&#8710;</italic>
###xml 244 249 244 249 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">ipt1&#8710;</italic>

###xml 283 288 283 288 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">sur1&#8710;</italic>

###xml 379 390 379 390 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">left column</italic>
###xml 435 447 435 447 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">right column</italic>
###xml 450 451 450 451 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">b</bold>

###end p 88
###begin title 89
Interaction Between Pdr10 Function and Sphingolipids
###end title 89
###begin p 90
###xml 124 128 124 128 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR6">2000</xref>
###xml 321 335 321 335 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S. cerevisiae,</italic>
###xml 496 500 496 500 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">AUR1</italic>
###xml 602 606 602 606 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR77">1997</xref>
###xml 750 754 750 754 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR9">1997</xref>
###xml 770 774 770 774 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR104">2003</xref>
###xml 850 851 850 851 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 903 907 903 907 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR25">1997</xref>
###xml 93 98 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 321 334 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
Sphingolipids are known to be important factors for lateral segregation and DRM formation in yeast membranes (Bagnat et al. 2000). We were therefore interested in the interplay between sphingolipid composition and Pdr10 function, because of its role as a negative regulator of Pdr12 association with the DRM fraction. In S. cerevisiae, the headgroups of mature sphingolipids contain inositol phosphate. Inositol phosphate is added to ceramide by Aur1, producing inositolphosphorylceramide (IPC). AUR1 is an essential gene, demonstrating that IPC or lipids derived from IPC are essential (Nagiec et al. 1997). IPC is converted to mannosyl-inositolphosphoryl-ceramide (MIPC) by the redundant action of Sur1 (also known as Csg1) and Csh1 (Beeler et al. 1997; Uemura et al. 2003). MIPC is then converted to mannosyl-di(inositolphosphoryl)-ceramide (M[IP]2C) by the subsequent action of Ipt1 (Dickson et al. 1997).
###end p 90
###begin p 91
###xml 5 10 5 10 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">sur1&#8710;</italic>
###xml 15 20 15 20 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">ipt1&#8710;</italic>
###xml 49 55 49 55 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 122 124 122 124 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig12" ref-type="fig">12</xref>

Both sur1∆ and ipt1∆ mutations were epistatic to pdr10∆ for both Calcofluor White sensitivity and sorbate resistant (Fig. 12a). Thus, deletion of genes required for synthesis of mature sphingolipid headgroups ablated the phenotypes of pdr10∆ cells, even though pdr10∆ cells did not exhibit significant changes either in the level of total phosphosphingolipid (Table 2) or in the composition of phosphosphingolipids (Table 4). However, sur1∆ and ipt1∆ cells exhibited significant changes in sphingolipid composition whether Pdr10 was present or absent, as expected (Table 4). pdr10∆ sur1∆ cells did not display abnormally high TNBS labeling of PE (Fig. 12b and Table 2) and contained normal levels of Pdr12 (Fig. 12c). Furthermore, both sur1∆ and ipt1∆ eliminated the elevated chitin deposition caused by a pdr10∆ mutation (Fig. 12d). Taken together, these results demonstrate that the aberrant accumulation of Pdr12 and defective endocytosis of Chs3 that occur in pdr10∆ cells require the presence of mannosylphosphosphingolipids.Table 4Composition of mature sphingolipidsStrainIPCMIPCM(IP)2CWild type35 +/- 417 +/- 247 +/- 4pdr10∆41 +/- 218 +/- 241 +/- 1sur1∆61 +/- 313 +/- 126 +/- 3ipt1∆46 +/- 348 +/- 27 +/- 1Note: Strains were W303-1A (wild type), NRY243 (pdr10∆), NRY409 (sur1∆), and NRY410 (ipt1∆). ###begin title 94
Discussion
###end title 94
###begin p 95
###xml 196 210 196 210 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S. cerevisiae.</italic>
###xml 499 504 499 504 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dnf1&#8710;</italic>
###xml 504 509 504 509 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dnf2&#8710;</italic>
###xml 619 623 619 623 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR84">2003</xref>
###xml 631 637 631 637 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 648 653 648 653 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dnf1&#8710;</italic>
###xml 653 658 653 658 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dnf2&#8710;</italic>
###xml 846 852 846 852 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 954 960 954 960 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 190 195 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 196 209 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 236 241 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 580 585 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
We have shown that the ABC transporter Pdr10 is a plasma membrane protein that plays a role in maintaining the proper distribution and function of a subset of other membrane proteins in the yeast S. cerevisiae. In the absence of Pdr10, yeast cells exhibit a modest defect in the endocytosis of the chitin synthase Chs3, resulting in increased cell wall chitin and consequent sensitivity to Calcofluor White. The increased chitin deposition and Calcofluor sensitivity are equivalent to those seen in dnf1∆dnf2∆ cells, which lack the putative inward-directed PE translocases of the yeast plasma membrane (Pomorski et al. 2003). Both pdr10∆ cells and dnf1∆dnf2∆ cells exhibit increased accessibility of PE to TNBS labeling, consistent with a defect in maintenance of plasma membrane asymmetry. However, Dnf1 and Dnf2 are present at normal levels in pdr10∆ cells, and these cells are able to internalize NBD-PE. It thus seems likely that these phenotypes of pdr10∆ cells arise indirectly, perhaps as a consequence of a modest defect in trafficking of plasma membrane proteins.
###end p 95
###begin p 96
###xml 238 244 238 244 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 308 313 308 313 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">PDR12</italic>
###xml 424 430 424 430 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 558 564 558 564 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 613 626 613 626 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">PDR10, PDR12,</italic>
###xml 631 636 631 636 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">LEM3,</italic>
###xml 645 651 645 651 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr12&#8710;</italic>
###xml 668 674 668 674 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 737 742 737 742 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">lem3&#8710;</italic>
###xml 759 765 759 765 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 807 818 807 818 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dnf1&#8710; dnf2&#8710;</italic>
###xml 850 856 850 856 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 917 935 917 935 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710; dnf1&#8710; dnf2&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1032 1037 1032 1037 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr5&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1054 1060 1054 1060 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1198 1203 1198 1203 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">sur1&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1208 1214 1208 1214 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">ipt1&#8710;,</italic>
###xml 1302 1308 1302 1308 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1446 1452 1446 1452 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 135 140 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
The absence of Pdr10 function also results in increased resistance to the weak organic acid sorbate. Sorbate is directly exported from yeast cells by the plasma membrane ABC transporter Pdr12, itself a Pdr10 paralogue. We have shown that pdr10∆ cells accumulate higher levels of Pdr12, despite no changes in PDR12 transcription. Additionally, we have shown that the behavior of Pdr12 upon detergent extraction is altered in pdr10∆ membranes: while Pdr12 in wild-type membranes is mainly in the detergent-soluble fraction, it is mainly in the DRM fraction in pdr10∆ membranes. There are complex interactions among PDR10, PDR12, and LEM3, because pdr12∆ is epistatic to pdr10∆ for Calcofluor sensitivity and for TNBS labeling of PE, while lem3∆ is epistatic to pdr10∆ for sorbate resistance. By contrast, the dnf1∆ dnf2∆ double mutant is equivalent to pdr10∆ for Chs3-related phenotypes, with no additive effect in the pdr10∆ dnf1∆ dnf2∆ triple mutant, but does not affect the Pdr12-related phenotype of sorbate resistance. Moreover, pdr5∆ is epistatic to pdr10∆ for both phenotypes. Pdr5, Pdr12, and Lem3 thus seem to be proteins intimately linked to the molecular function of Pdr10. Interestingly, sur1∆ and ipt1∆, which lack genes required for elaboration of sphingolipid headgroups, are epistatic to pdr10∆ for Calcofluor sensitivity, chitin deposition, sorbate resistance, Pdr12 accumulation, and TNBS labeling of PE. The aberrant behavior of pdr10∆ membranes thus requires at least three specific proteins (Pdr5, Pdr12, and Lem3) and also requires mature mannosylphosphosphingolipids.
###end p 96
###begin p 97
###xml 37 43 37 43 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 135 153 135 153 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">negative regulator</italic>
###xml 420 424 420 424 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR36">2003</xref>
###xml 502 508 502 508 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 558 559 558 559 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab3" ref-type="table">3</xref>
###xml 925 929 925 929 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR36">2003</xref>
###xml 1122 1128 1122 1128 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1222 1228 1222 1228 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
In the presence of these components, pdr10∆ cells significantly overincorporate Pdr12 into the DRM fraction. Pdr10 thus functions as a negative regulator of Pdr12 incorporation into DRMs. Slight increases in Pdr5 and Lem3 association with the DRM fraction are also seen, while Gas1 and Chs3 distributions are unchanged. Pdr12 has been estimated to be present at approximately 750 molecules per cell (Ghaemmaghami et al. 2003), and approximately one-third of the total is shifted to the DRM fraction in pdr10∆ membranes relative to wild-type membranes (Table 3). Therefore, approximately 250 additional Pdr12 molecules per cell would become incorporated into the DRM fraction in the absence of Pdr10. In the same study, levels of Ste20 were estimated at approximately 260 molecules per cell, and both Dnf1 and Dnf2 exhibited lower but still detectable signals; however, Pdr10 could not be detected at all (Ghaemmaghami et al. 2003). Therefore, Pdr10 levels must be substantially lower than 250 molecules per cell, which means that Pdr10 is substoichiometric with the Pdr12 population that is shifted to the DRM fraction in pdr10∆ membranes. It thus seems unlikely that the overincorporation of Pdr12 in the DRM fraction of pdr10∆ membranes reflects the loss of a stoichiometric association between Pdr10 and Pdr12.
###end p 97
###begin p 98
###xml 317 321 317 321 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR64">2006</xref>
###xml 338 342 338 342 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR65">2007</xref>
###xml 776 782 776 782 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1047 1053 1047 1053 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1568 1570 1568 1570 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig10" ref-type="fig">10</xref>
###xml 1797 1803 1795 1801 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1939 1945 1937 1943 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 2071 2077 2069 2075 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
One could also envision the redistribution of Pdr12 into the DRM fraction as arising from an endocytic defect, such that an intracellular pool of Pdr12 would effectively relocalize to the plasma membrane in the absence of Pdr10. The proposed link between endocytosis and exit from the DRM fraction (Lauwers and Andre 2006; Lauwers et al. 2007) would imply that such an intracellular pool would become available for partitioning into the DRM fraction upon such relocalization. In this model, the plasma membrane Pdr12 would be in equilibrium between a microenvironment that gives rise to a DRM fraction and a microenvironment that does not, while intracellular Pdr12 would instead be entirely associated with the detergent-soluble fraction. The absence of Pdr12 endocytosis in pdr10∆ cells would then result in the total Pdr12 population equilibrating between these two microenvironments, rather than just a plasma membrane population doing so. Were this to be the case, the association of approximately 60% of total Pdr12 with the DRM fraction in pdr10∆ cells would reflect the intrinsic affinity of Pdr12 for this fraction, while no intracellular Pdr12 would be found in the DRM fraction. The amount of Pdr12 in the DRM fraction would thus vary between 0% and 60%. The association of approximately 30% of total Pdr12 with the DRM fraction in wild-type cells would thus imply the presence of approximately 50% of total Pdr12 in an intracellular pool. However, we demonstrated that a functional Pdr12-GFP chimera is predominantly localized to the plasma membrane (Fig. 10b). Only </= 30% of cells exhibited any detectable intracellular Pdr12-GFP, and the vast majority of such cells also displayed plasma membrane Pdr12-GFP. Moreover, this localization pattern was equivalent in both wild-type and pdr10∆ cells. Therefore, the intracellular population of Pdr12 is likely to be much less than 50% of the total, and there is no evidence that pdr10∆ cells lack this population. These observations are inconsistent with the idea that Pdr12 accumulation in the DRM fraction of pdr10∆ membranes arises from relocalization of Pdr12 to the plasma membrane. We favor, therefore, the idea that the composition of plasma membrane in cells lacking Pdr10 is itself different from that of the wild-type plasma membrane in a way that results in overincorporation of Pdr12 into the DRM fraction.
###end p 98
###begin p 99
###xml 526 530 526 530 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR69">2005</xref>
###xml 731 737 731 737 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 170 175 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
The gradient flotation assay we used to examine DRM incorporation does not provide information about the actual size of any detergent-resistant structures present in the yeast plasma membrane, but is only sensitive to density during fractionation. It is not rigorously established whether microdomains of native membranes can be stably maintained during the lysis, extraction, and flotation procedure, and it has been argued that detergent treatment can induce DRM formation in homogeneous model membranes (Lichtenberg et al. 2005). However, it is hard to see how such an effect could be specifically enhanced by the absence of such a rare protein as Pdr10. Therefore, the higher occupancy of Pdr12 in the DRM fraction observed in pdr10∆ membranes would represent either its increased partitioning into a specialized membrane subdomain or its increased interaction with boundary lipids that are resistant to removal by detergent. It thus seems that the microenvironment of Pdr12 in the native membrane is altered in the absence of Pdr10.
###end p 99
###begin p 100
###xml 589 595 589 595 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 1197 1201 1197 1201 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR36">2003</xref>
###xml 1289 1290 1289 1290 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab3" ref-type="table">3</xref>
The most parsimonious model to accommodate all of the observations we have made is that the normal function of Pdr10 is to counteract some of the protein or lipid movements that are needed to establish a specific microdomain in the plasma membrane. Hence, when Pdr10 is absent, there is aberrant or excess formation of this microdomain, and Pdr12 becomes sequestered. Chs3 and Gas1 do not get trapped in this way. Pdr12 sequestered in such a compartment may be more resistant to the processes responsible for its endocytosis, explaining why it accumulates to a higher than normal level in pdr10∆ cells. If Pdr12, Lem3, and Pdr5 are all required for formation of this microdomain, the observed epistasis among these membrane proteins would be explained, because removal of a required microdomain component would disrupt the microdomain and mimic the loss of a negative regulator. Similarly, if the proposed microdomain regulated by Pdr10 requires mature sphingolipids for its formation, then alteration of sphingolipid composition could also ameliorate the effects resulting from the absence of Pdr10, as we observed. Lem3 and Pdr5 are both present at higher levels than Pdr12 (Ghaemmaghami et al. 2003), which provides a possible explanation for why their shift to the DRM fraction (Table 3) is not statistically significant: if Pdr12 is also required for microdomain formation and is less abundant than Lem3 or Pdr5, it will be limiting for microdomain formation, such that the amount of Lem3 and Pdr5 shifted to the DRM fraction is smaller.
###end p 100
###begin p 101
###xml 177 183 177 183 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 354 358 354 358 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR89">2007</xref>
###xml 504 513 504 513 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">accretion</italic>
###xml 555 564 555 564 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">aggregate</italic>
###xml 761 765 761 765 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR40">2007</xref>
###xml 966 970 966 970 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR6">2000</xref>
###xml 1000 1004 1000 1004 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR27">2003</xref>
###xml 1019 1023 1019 1023 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR44">2003</xref>
###xml 1148 1152 1148 1152 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR7">2001</xref>
###xml 1165 1169 1165 1169 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR66">2002</xref>
###xml 1346 1350 1346 1350 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR85">1994</xref>
###xml 1362 1366 1362 1366 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR113">2007</xref>
###xml 1447 1455 1447 1455 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">positive</italic>
###xml 1575 1583 1575 1583 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">negative</italic>
###xml 866 871 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 1341 1345 <span type="species:ncbi:163112">Ling</span>
The precise nature of the putative sphingolipid-dependent microdomain on which Pdr10 has its effect is unclear. Pdr12, Dnf1, Dnf2, Lem3, and Pdr5 do not accumulate in the ER in pdr10∆ cells, and we have shown that Pdr12 and Pdr5 are active in these cells. The absence of Pdr10 also does not activate the unfolded-protein response pathway (Ron and Walter 2007) (data not shown), providing further evidence that these membrane proteins are not misfolded. Therefore, this microdomain should be viewed as an accretion of functional proteins rather than as an aggregate of unfolded proteins. It has been reported that association of permeases into ~300-nm patches in the membrane requires maintenance of the transmembrane electrochemical potential (Grossmann et al. 2007). Also, it is now generally accepted that segregation of membrane proteins into the DRM fraction in yeast and other eukaryotes depends on the presence of both sterols and sphingolipids (Bagnat et al. 2000; Dupre and Haguenauer-Tsapis 2003; Hearn et al. 2003). Formation of large oligomers of Pma1 is required for the segregation of this protein into the DRM fraction (Bagnat et al. 2001; Lee et al. 2002), and there is ample evidence for certain members of the ABC transporter family that their native state in the plasma membrane is as a large homomultimer (Poruchynsky and Ling 1994; Xu et al. 2007). Thus, both protein-protein and lipid-protein interactions can contribute in a positive sense to microdomain formation, and indeed our epistasis analysis provides indirect evidence for both. Pdr10 acts as a negative regulator of the establishment and/or maintenance of plasma membrane microdomains. This suggests that microdomain assembly and breakdown are quite dynamic processes in vivo.
###end p 101
###begin p 102
###xml 129 135 129 135 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 405 418 405 418 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 718 724 718 724 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">pdr10&#8710;</italic>
###xml 815 820 815 820 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">PDR10</italic>
###xml 877 890 877 890 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 917 921 917 921 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR33">2005</xref>
###xml 405 418 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 877 890 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 957 962 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 1391 1396 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
If microdomains have a critically important role in the physiological functions of the plasma membrane, one might question why a pdr10∆ mutant is viable. However, all of the evidence we have presented indicates that microdomain formation still occurs in cells lacking Pdr10 and that the defect is in the efficiency of microdomain disassembly. Moreover, there are yet other ABC transporters encoded in the S. cerevisiae genome whose function may be semiredundant with that of Pdr10 and, hence, partially mask the true effect of the problems that might be caused if microdomains remained too static. The latter consideration may explain why we observed some strain-background dependence in the phenotypes conferred by a pdr10∆ mutation. In this regard, it is also interesting to note that the noncoding region of the PDR10 gene has been identified as a mutational hotspot in the S. cerevisiae genome (Fay and Benavides 2005). Thus, different lineages of this yeast may have adapted to cope with different demands for Pdr10 function. It is also important to note that the functional significance of the putative Pdr10-regulated microdomain is unclear; it is quite possible that this structure is actually a pathological association of functional Pdr12, Pdr5, and Lem3 in the presence of sphingolipids, and we do not propose to identify it with one of the previously defined compartments of the yeast plasma membrane.
###end p 102
###begin p 103
The function of Pdr10 at the molecular level and the means by which its biochemical activity contravenes microdomain formation or maintenance have yet to be elucidated. Possibly, Pdr10 catalyzes outward translocation of an as yet unidentified lipid substrate, by analogy to its paralogous ABC transporter, Pdr5. Alternatively, the fact that Pdr10 itself localizes in discrete puncta in the plasma membrane may be important. For example, the microdomain compartment that appears to accumulate when Pdr10 is absent might be formed from (or beside) the subdomain normally occupied by Pdr10 itself. Hence, Pdr10 could help to occlude or exclude other integral membrane proteins from this same region without invoking any need for it to have transport activity. Another possibility is that, when Pdr10 is embedded in the plasma membrane, it dynamically associates with a specific set of lipids (or even other proteins); in this way, Pdr10 may act as a "detergent" to "solubilize" these components and thus enhance their dispersal within the plane of the membrane.
###end p 103
###begin p 104
